        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b185de39-0be0-4a7d-9475-0560be84d5aa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d55730407" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion hybrid supercapacitors (Na-HSCs) by virtue of synergizing the merits of batteries and supercapacitors have attracted considerable attention for high-energy and high-power energy-storage applications. Orthorhombic Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (<i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) has recently been recognized as a promising anode material for Na-HSCs due to its typical pseudocapacitive feature, but it suffers from intrinsically low electrical conductivity. Reasonably high electrochemical performance of <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-based electrodes could merely be gained to date when sufficient carbon content was introduced. In addition, flexible Na-HSC devices have scarcely been demonstrated by far. Herein, an in situ encapsulation strategy is devised to directly grow ultrathin graphene shells over <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires (denoted as Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites) by plasma-enhanced chemical vapor deposition, targeting a highly conductive anode material for Na-HSCs. The few-layered graphene capsules with ample topological defects would enable facile electron and Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport, guaranteeing rapid pseudocapacitive processes at the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/electrolyte interface. The Na-HSC full-cell comprising a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an activated carbon cathode delivers high energy/power densities (112.9 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>/80.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup> and 62.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>/5330 W kg<sup>−1</sup>), outperforming those of recently reported Na-HSC counterparts. Proof-of-concept Na-HSC devices with favorable mechanical robustness manifest stable electrochemical performances under different bending conditions and after various bending–release cycles.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201800963-sec-0010">
         
         <p>The present energy-storage landscape continues to be dominated by lithium-ion batteries owing to their high energy densities (150–300 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and satisfying lifespans (500–1000 cycles).<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0001_R_d55730399e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">1</a>-<a href="#adma201800963-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0003_R_d55730399e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></span> However, the limited power densities (&lt;1000 W kg<sup>−1</sup>) are insufficient to meet the ever-increasing demands for the next-generation energy-storage devices. By contrast, electrochemical capacitors (ECs) are capable of delivering high power density (&gt;10<sup>4</sup> W kg<sup>−1</sup>) with favorable durability (10<sup>4</sup>–10<sup>5</sup> cycles), yet suffer from relatively low energy densities (&lt;10 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0004_R_d55730399e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adma201800963-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0007_R_d55730399e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></span> In response, great research efforts have been devoted to construct lithium-ion hybrid supercapacitors/sodium-ion hybrid supercapacitors (Li-HSCs and Na-HSCs), by means of combining the working mechanism of a high-energy battery anode and a high-power EC cathode to synergize advantageous features from both.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0008_R_d55730399e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0009_R_d55730399e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></span> Specifically, Na-HSCs have attracted growing attention in recent years due to the low-cost and large natural abundance of Na, as well as superior ionic conductivity of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions compared to that of solvated Li<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0010_R_d55730399e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a>-<a href="#adma201800963-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0014_R_d55730399e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span></p>
         
         <p>The key to high-performance Na-HSCs lies in seeking appropriate electrode materials to surmount the kinetic imbalance between the sluggish faradic reaction in anode and fast non-faradic adsorption/desorption process in cathode. In order to overcome the limited Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion within the battery-type anode, considerable efforts have been made throughout the introduction of nanoarchitectured electrodes, as well as the exploration of new materials with pseudocapacitive behaviors and sizable Na<sup>+</sup> tunnels in crystal structures. Inspired by their wide applications in Li/Na-ion batteries, carbonaceous materials, including hard carbon and bioderived carbon, have been preferred as anode candidates for Na-HSCs attaining good power capability.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0009_R_d55730399e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></span> Nevertheless, these high-surface-area carbon species still fail, to some extent, in delivering high energy density and volumetric capacity. As such, pseudocapacitive anode materials, such as metal oxides (e.g., V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0015_R_d55730399e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></span> NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0016_R_d55730399e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochim. Acta">16</a></span> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP),<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0017_R_d55730399e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></span> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0018_R_d55730399e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></span> etc.) and conductive MXene (e.g., Ti<sub>2</sub>C,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0019_R_d55730399e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a></span> Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub><span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0020_R_d55730399e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></span>) nanosheets, have been widely investigated to compensate the energy density performances targeting advanced Na-HSCs.</p>
         
         <p>Recent years have witnessed a growing interest in employing orthorhombic Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (<i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) as an anode material for Na-ion batteries and Na-HSCs.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0021_R_d55730399e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#adma201800963-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0024_R_d55730399e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></span> This mainly benefits from the large interplanar lattice spacing of 3.9 Å with its (001) planes<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0025_R_d55730399e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></span> that could accommodate large amounts of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (ion radius: 1.06 Å)<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0026_R_d55730399e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></span> and endow fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. More significantly, <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was found to demonstrate a rapid pseudocapacitive response in a potential range of 0.01–2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), which would be promising for retaining synchronous high-energy and high-power-density.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0027_R_d55730399e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></span> Nevertheless, the low electrical conductivity of <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> poses a daunting threat to its anode designs in a practical manner. As such, reasonably high electrochemical performance could merely be gained when sufficient carbon content was introduced. For instance, Lim et al. designed a novel Na-HSC anode comprising Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@amorphous carbon core–shell particles and reduced graphene oxide (Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@C-rGO), managing to achieve a high energy density of ≈76 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> when the rGO content was up to 50 wt%.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0028_R_d55730399e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></span> Very recently, Tong et al. developed a sandwich-like Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/graphene/Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode with a rGO content of 13.8 wt%, delivering favorable energy densities of 56.1 and 9.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> under 120 and 7200 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0029_R_d55730399e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></span> Apparently, the large portion of graphene could contribute to the calculated energy density of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, yet giving rise to a lower total mass energy density. In addition, the existence of abundant graphene framework might hinder the effective Na<sup>+</sup> transport to Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> surface, thereby impeding the reaction kinetics. In this sense, further advances in architecture design and preparation technology of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/graphene composites are desirable to boost the energy and power density of Na-HSCs to a higher level.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we devise an in situ encapsulation strategy to directly grow ultrathin graphene shells over <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires (denoted as Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites) via plasma-enhanced chemical vapor deposition (PECVD) as the durable anode materials for Na-HSC devices. Distinct from the traditional amorphous carbon coatings and rGO wrappings, our PECVD-derived graphene shell exhibits highly tunable defect density, endowing the inner Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires with excellent and robust electrical conductivity. The ultrathin graphene shells with ample topological defects would also enable a quick electrolyte penetration and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport, guaranteeing rapid pseudocapacitive processes on the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/electrolyte interface. As for the Na half-cell, the as-obtained Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites display a high reversible capacity of ≈285 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.25 C and high-rate capability of ≈130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C, outperforming those of the commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> powders. More significantly, our Na-HSC full-cell comprising a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an activated carbon cathode delivers high energy/power densities (112.9 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>/80.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup> and 62.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>/5330 W kg<sup>−1</sup>) with a stable cycle life in the potential range of 1.0–4.3 V. As a proof of concept, bendable Na-HSC devices based on the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode manifest favorable mechanical and electrochemical properties upon cycling under deformed states, managing to lighten up a white light-emitting diode (LED) at different bending angles (0°, 180°, −180°). Considering that the development of flexible Na-HSC devices is by far in its infancy, our work marks a substantial progress toward the potential applications of high-energy, high power Na-HSCs in wearable energy storages.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>a illustrates the synthetic procedure of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. First, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires were synthesized with niobium powders and sodium hydroxide solution serving as precursors. Upon pouring them into a Teflon-lined autoclave, the mixture underwent a wet chemistry process to form sodium niobate (NaNbO<sub>3</sub>) nanowires (Figure S1, Supporting Information). Accordingly, the intermediate NaNbO<sub>3</sub> product after thorough washing experienced a hydrogen ion exchange procedure in diluted HCl overnight. The precipitate was collected, washed three times, freeze-dried, and finally annealed at 700 °C in air to produce <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires. These nanowires with uniform widths of 100–150 nm and lengths of several micrometers are stacked in a fluffy manner (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>b). The thus-obtained <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires were then directly placed into a CVD furnace as growth substrates for the conformal coating of graphene. To prevent the reduction of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> at H<sub>2</sub> ambients and/or under elevated growth temperatures (Figure S2, Supporting Information), a facile PECVD route was adopted by introducing a remote plasma generator with the employment of Ar/CH<sub>4</sub> flow. Upon experiencing a low-temperature (500 °C) CVD reaction for 30–60 min (Figure S3, Supporting Information), the white <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires turned dark grey to the naked eye (Figure S4, Supporting Information). The wire-shaped morphology of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is found to be well preserved after PECVD, where almost all the nanowires are uniformly covered with graphene shells (highlighted by yellow arrows, Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>c). The preservation of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowire structure would be beneficial to facilitating the ion diffusion path. It should be noted that the associated energy cost and scalability of such Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> hybrids is still a big concern to meet the practical Na-HSC applications at the current stage.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0030_R_d55730399e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Chem.">30</a></span> Designing a new synthetic procedure that combines the thermal annealing and PECVD growth is currently ongoing in our lab, in which the intermediate NaNbO<sub>3</sub> product will be first annealed at 700 °C in Ar/O<sub>2</sub> mixture and then in situ encapsulated by graphene via switching to Ar/CH<sub>4</sub> flow at 500 °C. Furthermore, establishing scalable and rotatable PECVD systems would indeed allow continuous batch introduction, in turn, the comprehensive cost might be remarkably reduced in future. Detailed inspection of an individual Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanostructure using transmission electron microscope (TEM) discloses that Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowire is dominantly coated by ultrathin graphene capsules as well as a few vertically interspersed graphene nanowalls (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>d). High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of a typical graphene/Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> interface demonstrates the high crystallinity of inner Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> core possessing a lattice fringe of ≈3.9 Å, in good agreement with the spacing of the (001) plane (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>e). It is also evident that the uniformly coated graphene on the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> phase is of few-layer nature. Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0001">1</a>f additionally shows the morphology of an erected graphene nanowall, possessing a relatively lower crystallinity. The growth of graphene nanowalls along the vertical direction has been ubiquitously observed in the low-temperature PECVD process, which was speculated to be due to the guidance of the localized electric field.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0031_R_d55730399e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Res.">31</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0032_R_d55730399e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></span> From another perspective, the existence of these amorphous graphene nanowalls may further increase the topological defects and create more channels for Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. The distribution of the elements of interest in the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite was mapped out using energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) under scanning TEM mode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0002">2</a>a–d). Apparently, strong C signal can be found in the graphene shell and the erected graphene wall, while Nb and O elements are homogeneously distributed within the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> core. This serves as a solid evidence with regard to the uniform capsulation of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires by PECVD-derived graphene.</p>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201800963-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/190b1edc-d60b-4556-bc96-ebe270f5c36d/adma201800963-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/190b1edc-d60b-4556-bc96-ebe270f5c36d/adma201800963-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1288d140-fabb-45f4-9848-e1cb23b4201c/adma201800963-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/190b1edc-d60b-4556-bc96-ebe270f5c36d/adma201800963-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201800963-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201800963" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) TEM image and corresponding EDS mapping of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. e) XRD patterns of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. f) Raman spectra of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. g) High-resolution Nb 3d XPS spectra of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. h) High-resolution O 1s XPS signals of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The high crystallinity and phase purity of <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires in the presence and absence of CVD-grown graphene was examined by X-ray diffraction (XRD). All the diffraction peaks at 22.6° (001), 28.3° (180), 28.9° (200), 36.5° (181), and 46.0° (002) from both XRD profiles accord well with the orthorhombic Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> crystals (JCPDS No. 30–0873),<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0027_R_d55730399e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></span> revealing that the PECVD procedure does not affect the bulk phase of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. In particular, there are no discernible peaks at 26.6° for G-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which manifests that our CVD route has enabled the effective prevention of the stacking of graphene layers (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0002">2</a>e). To verify the CVD formation of graphene on Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, Raman spectroscopy was applied. Bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> displays Raman vibrational modes centered at 229, 308, and 689 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0002">2</a>f), corresponding to the vibrations of cations inside octahedra and tetrahedra, as well as the stretching Nb<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0033_R_d55730399e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci. Rep.">33</a></span> As for the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite, featured graphene signals in terms of the D band (1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>), G band (1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>), and 2D band (2700 cm<sup>−1</sup>) can be observed, implying the successful graphene coating with few-layer nature and high defect densities, in good agreement with the HRTEM analysis. Moreover, one strong peak at ≈675 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the symmetric stretching mode of niobia polyhedra merely appears, which was normally found in the disordered Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> phase.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0033_R_d55730399e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci. Rep.">33</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0034_R_d55730399e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Mater.">34</a></span> The marked differences in the Raman spectra between <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> can be ascribed to the surface chemistry modification of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> outermost surface during the PECVD reaction. In this respect, the plasma bombardment might result in the loss of surface elements (Nb and O) and possible implantation of foreign ions (H and C), thereby modifying the lattice arrangements at the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> surface to a certain extent.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to probe the chemical constitution of as-obtained products, with the full-scan spectrum of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite displaying Nb, O, and C signals (Figure S5, Supporting Information). The high-resolution Nb 3d spectrum of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires presents Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peak at 207.1 eV and Nb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> peak at 209.8 eV, which is consistent with the binding energies (BE) of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0002">2</a>g). The Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite exhibits relevant peaks locating at 207.7 eV (Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) and 210.5 eV (Nb 3d<sub>3/2</sub>), showing a slight shift to higher binding energy as compared to that of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Previous report by Atuchin et al. pointed out that the BE difference ∆(O<span class="icomoon"></span>Nb) (∆(O<span class="icomoon"></span>Nb) = BE(O 1s) − BE(Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub>)) is a reliable parameter to characterize electronic properties of the niobate instead of BE(Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub>).<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0034_R_d55730399e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0035_R_d55730399e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electron Spectrosc. Relat. Phenom.">35</a></span> For instance, a ∆(O<span class="icomoon"></span>Nb) value of 323.0–323.1 eV can be ascribed to Nb<sup>5+</sup> in various phases of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. In this sense, both the <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> herein show the identical ∆(O<span class="icomoon"></span>Nb) value of 323.0 eV, revealing that the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is still the dominant species in the composites after the graphene capsulation. Furthermore, Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0002">2</a>h compares the core-level O 1s signals of the <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites. The Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite exhibits three peaks centered at 530.7, 531.8, and 533 eV, corresponding to the bulk Nb<span class="icomoon"></span>O, Nb<span class="icomoon"></span>OH, and surface oxygen, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0022_R_d55730399e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></span> The presence of Nb<span class="icomoon"></span>OH could be attributed to the H atom implantation during the PECVD process, where the noticeable peak shift toward higher binding energy for the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> might originate from the existence of Nb<sub>2</sub><i><sub>-x</sub></i>O<sub>5</sub><i><sub>-y</sub></i>H<i><sub>z</sub></i> at the surface of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> due to the loss of Nb and O elements. The good consistency of these characterization results suggests that the direct coating of <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires with graphene shells via PECVD has exerted a negligible impact upon the bulk phase of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which is expected to lead to exceptional Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage properties.</p>
         
         <p>To probe the effects of graphene capsulation on the sodium storage behaviors of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, as-fabricated Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites, bare Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires, and commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> powders as anodes were assembled into coin cells with metallic Na serving as counter electrodes. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>a, the first-cycle charge capacity of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode at 0.25 C is evaluated to be 284.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency of 32.8%. Such a low first Coulombic efficiency is in principle due to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase and irreversible side reactions/ion trappings inside the nanostructured electrode materials.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0022_R_d55730399e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0028_R_d55730399e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></span> After 30 cycles, a high reversible capacity of ≈229.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved at the same current density with a remarkable capacity retention of 80.5%, outperforming those of bare Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowire (182.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 67.9%) and commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (92.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 63.6%) based electrodes (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>b). The low initial capacity and fast capacity decay of the commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> might be due to its sizable powder dimensions to induce lengthy ion-diffusion paths (Figure S6, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0024_R_d55730399e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></span> Moreover, the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode exhibits advanced rate capability at current densities from 0.25 to 20 C, with a reversible capacity of ≈156.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> retained even at 20 C (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>c). This is apparently superior to that of bare Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires (110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 20 C) and commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (65.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 10 C). The noticeable difference in the cycling stability and rate capability between the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes can be ascribed to the key roles played by graphene capsules: i) to retain the structural integrity of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite, ii) to guarantee a robust electrical framework upon cycling, and iii) to enable a quick electrolyte penetration and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport. This can be further confirmed by the Nyquist plots of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes after three formation cycles (Figure S7, Supporting Information). As such, Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode exhibits much lower charge-transfer resistance than that of bare <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode, demonstrating the enhanced kinetics of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode. Note that our Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite is featured by an ultralow carbon content (&lt;1%) to realize a fast and durable Na-ion storage, which is in stark contrast to the scenarios of reported Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@C nanocomposites with carbon contents of no less than 10 wt%.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0028_R_d55730399e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0029_R_d55730399e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></span> In this sense, the PECVD-derived graphene capsulated Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> hybrids could deliver an advanced mass energy density. Deep cycling of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode was further carried out at high rates of 10 and 20 C up to 1000 cycles. It is evident that stable capacities of 153–171 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C (Figure S8, Supporting Information) and 133–147 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>d) can be obtained without distinct decay upon cycling, demonstrating its excellent high-rate cyclability for sodium storage regardless of slight capacity fluctuation. This corroborates the advantageous ability of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites with respect to the fast electron and Na-ion transports. The capacity fluctuation could be attributed to the short interval time set between the two adjacent cycles. Normally, sufficient interval time is necessary for the structural recovery of the electrode upon sodiation/desodiation. A relatively short interval time may result in the capacity variation in a small range. Additionally, it is worth noting that although the reversible capacity of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode is comparable with that of many other hybrid graphene composite electrodes (e.g., MoS<sub>2</sub>/graphene),<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0036_R_d55730399e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a></span> it is still inferior to that of Sn/graphene, Pb/graphene, Sb/graphene, and P/graphene composite electrodes.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0037_R_d55730399e1325" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. C">37</a></span> Nevertheless, these achievement of high capacity might cause severe volume change during sodiation, thus greatly shortening the life span, especially at high current densities (e.g., 20 C). By contrast, our Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode maintained an areal capacity of 0.16–0.176 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at a high rate of 20 C after ≈1000 cycles, indicative of its suitability for constructing high-rate and long-life Na-ion batteries. In this respect, the capacity contribution from our PECVD-derived graphene capsules is negligible due to its extremely low mass content (&lt;1%) and intrinsically limited Na-ion storage capability (&lt;100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0036_R_d55730399e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0037_R_d55730399e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. C">37</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201800963-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9564afe-ebb8-4ebb-86e6-8135677baa3b/adma201800963-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9564afe-ebb8-4ebb-86e6-8135677baa3b/adma201800963-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/68891e67-db06-4645-94cc-0a84883837e9/adma201800963-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9564afe-ebb8-4ebb-86e6-8135677baa3b/adma201800963-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201800963-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201800963" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites: a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode at various current densities from 0.25 to 20 C. The first discharge–charge curves at 0.25 C were used to calculate the initial Coulombic efficiency. b) Cycling performance of commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes at 0.25 C rate. c) Rate capabilities of commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes at various current densities from 0.25 to 20 C. d) Long-term electrochemical cycling of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode at 20 C rate. e) CV curves of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode at various scan rates from 0.1 to 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. f) Plot of log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) of anodic peaks (blue circles) and b-value determination lines according to the relationship: <i>i = av<sup>b</sup></i>, in which this value is 0.97 up to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and 0.42 at scan rates &gt;10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. g) TEM image of the graphene capsules after removal of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> cores.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain insight into the favorable rate performance of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite anodes, cyclic voltammetry (CV) at various scan rates from 0.1 to 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.01–3 V was performed, as displayed in Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>e. The detailed CV curves at the scan rates from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup> are further presented in Figure S9 (Supporting Information). The measured current <i>i</i> obeys a power-law relationship with the sweep rate <i>v</i>, <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable values; <i>b</i> = 0.5 indicates a semi-infinite diffusion process, and <i>b</i> = 1 corresponds to a capacitive process.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0038_R_d55730399e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Mater.">38</a></span> In this regard, the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation kinetics could be analyzed by plotting log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) for cathodic or anodic peak currents. With respect to the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite anode, the <i>b</i> value for the anodic current in a sweep rate range of 0.1–10 mV s<sup>−1</sup> is quantified as ≈0.97 (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>f), suggesting fast surface-controlled kinetics. Considering the unique core/shell structure of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composite, such a high <i>b</i> value demonstrates a facile Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation into the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> core occurred at relatively slow rates throughout the graphene shell. Indeed, our PECVD-derived graphene shell with abundant defects would enable a quick electrolyte penetration, guaranteeing rapid pseudocapacitive processes at Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. This can be verified by a designed etching experiment of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites using a dilute hydrofluoric acid solution. It can be observed that the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> core was completely removed after a short etching period (e.g., 1 h), with merely the graphene capsules remained (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0003">3</a>g; Figure S10, Supporting Information). By contrast, when the scan rates increase to above 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, a lower <i>b</i> value of 0.42 is obtained, corresponding to a diffusion-controlled process. This can be attributed to the diffusion constraint not only from the bulk electrolyte, but also through the “finite” defects within the graphene shells pertaining to an ultrafast scan rate.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">A closer examination of CV scan rate dependence using the method of Dunn and co-workers<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0039_R_d55730399e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Phys. Chem. C">39</a></span> further allowed us to quantitatively separate the contributions from surface capacitive effects and diffusion-controlled insertion reactions. To this end, the relationship <i>i = av<sup>b</sup></i> can be rewritten as
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201800963-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/bda41280-afeb-4cb9-aeb8-02017ebb2dd0/adma201800963-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>i</i> is the current at a fixed potential, and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are suitable values. By plotting <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> versus <i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be respectively quantified from the slope and the <i>y</i>-axis intercept point (Figure S11, Supporting Information). On a basis of this method, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>a presents that ≈61.2% of the total capacity of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites could be assigned to the capacitive contributions at a small scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, scanning at higher rates has seen that the surface capacitive effects become eventually pronounced due to the rapid charging/discharging associated with capacitive processes. As such, the capacitive contribution ratio even reaches a high value of 89.1% at 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>b; Figure S12, Supporting Information). Ex situ XRD analysis was performed to study the phase change of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode upon sodiation/desodiation in a half cell (Figure S13, Supporting Information). It is evident that Na<sup>+</sup> insertion occurred under a low current density (e.g., 0.1 C) could lead to distinct phase transition and even an amorphization of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> crystal at the end of discharging process.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0040_R_d55730399e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></span> Moreover, the phase transformation is irreversible in the following cycles due to the disordered crystal structure. Surprisingly, the amorphous phase was demonstrated to be capable of offering enhanced sodium storage properties due to higher ion diffusivity and larger lattice spacing.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0022_R_d55730399e1572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></span></div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201800963-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2213e0da-20cb-445f-ac59-681bbafb97e7/adma201800963-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2213e0da-20cb-445f-ac59-681bbafb97e7/adma201800963-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/49fb2ffe-1e33-4e73-8363-3d045ec027a3/adma201800963-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2213e0da-20cb-445f-ac59-681bbafb97e7/adma201800963-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201800963-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201800963" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curve of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode with separation between total current (solid line) and surface capacitive current (shaded regions) at 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) The capacitive contribution ratios of the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode at different scan rates from 0.1 to 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Schematic of the electron/Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport throughout the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. d) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the Na-HSC full cell employing a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an AC cathode at various current densities. e) Rate capability of the Na-HSC at various current densities from 0.03 to 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (the inset) and its long-term cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Ragone plots in comparison with results of other state-of-the-art Na-HSCs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Given the discussion above, our Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode indeed benefits from the uniform encapsulation of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires with defective graphene shells via PECVD, so that it manages to deliver an outstanding cycling stability and rate capability. The simple coating of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanostructures by such a mechanically robust, defect-engineered graphene could synergizing both improved electron transport and facile Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration properties of electrodes (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>c). In this sense, the facile PECVD route may act as an effective method for introducing conductive yet low-mass-loading additives for insulating pseudocapacitive nanomaterials toward scalable and practicable electrochemical applications.</p>
         
         <p>A Na-HSC full cell was constructed with a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an activated carbon cathode. Prior to assembling full cells, the commercial activated carbon (AC) with a surface area of 1350 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> was evaluated in half-cells with metallic sodium as both the counter and the reference electrodes (Figure S14, Supporting Information). In the potential range of 3.0–4.3 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, the specific capacity of AC cathode achieves ≈43 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S14b, Supporting Information). The linear galvanostatic charge–discharge curves demonstrate that AC cathode operates through non-faradic surface capacitive reaction. Moreover, the AC cathode exhibits superior rate capacity at high current densities from 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S14c, Supporting Information) and outstanding cycling stability up to 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S14d, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>d exhibits the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the Na-HSC full cell at different current densities. Note that the ideal weight ratio of the active anodic to cathodic materials was determined as 1:4 in this work according to the ragone plots of Na-HSCs using Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and AC cathode with respect to different weight ratios from 1:3.5 to 1:4.5 (Figure S15, Supporting Information). It can be clearly seen that the shape of the profiles, especially at low current rates, is somewhat deviated form the linear slope of an ideal supercapacitor. This can be ascribed to the specific sodium storage of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode consisting of both faradic and non-faradic reactions. The energy densities of the Na-HSC full cell at different current densities were calculated using equations shown in the Experimental Section according to the galvanostatic measurements. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>e and its inset, the Na-HSC full cell delivers the maximum energy densities of ≈112.9 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, with a power density of ≈80.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Note that the low Coulombic efficiency at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup> should be attributed to the irreversible side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte in the first cycle, and the charge/discharge profiles become quite symmetrical in the following two cycles (Figure S16, Supporting Information). Even at the high power density (≈5330 W kg<sup>−1</sup>) achieved at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, this Na-HSC system showed an energy density of ≈62.2 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>. More importantly, the Na-HSC full cell maintains a stable energy density of ≈67 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency above 97.1% for 1500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating its promising application foreground as high-energy and high-power energy storage devices with long life span.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0004">4</a>f displays the Ragone plot representing the trade-off between energy density and power density. In general, our assembled Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>//AC Na-HSC device could deliver a maximum energy density of 112.9 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 80.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high power density of 5330 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, the device still achieved a reasonably high energy density of 62.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, the electrochemical performance with respect to the energy-power characteristics of our Na-HSC is markedly superior to that of recently reported Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-based Na-HSC systems,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0021_R_d55730399e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0028_R_d55730399e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a>, <a href="#adma201800963-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0029_R_d55730399e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></span> as well as compares favorably with many state-of-the-art Na-HSC devices, such as Na-TiO<sub>2</sub>//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0041_R_d55730399e1720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></span> V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/carbon nanotube (CNT)//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0015_R_d55730399e1726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></span> NVP//NVP,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0017_R_d55730399e1728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></span> N-doped TiO<sub>2</sub>//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0042_R_d55730399e1733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></span> WO<sub>3</sub>@rGO//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0043_R_d55730399e1737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">43</a></span> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0044_R_d55730399e1748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Power Sources">44</a></span> CoHCF//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0045_R_d55730399e1750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Energy">45</a></span> MoS<sub>2</sub>/CF//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0046_R_d55730399e1754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Energy">46</a></span> TiO<sub>2</sub>-CNT@C//AC,<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0047_R_d55730399e1759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></span> and TiO<sub>2</sub>-Gr//AC.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0048_R_d55730399e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Nano">48</a></span> This again corroborates that our Na-HSC device based on a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an AC cathode is potentially promising for future energy storage applications.</p>
         
         <p>To envisage the applications of our Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites in wearable energy storage fields, bendable Na-HSC devices based on our Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and commercial AC cathode were accordingly assembled. Shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0005">5</a>a is a schematic illustration of the constructed bendable Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>//AC Na-HSC device. In turn, the effect of bending states on the electrochemistry performances of the full Na-HSC was probed. Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0005">5</a>b presents the charging–discharging curves of the bendable Na-HSC at different bending angles. Our device can be charged to 4.3 V, in contrast to ≈3.0 V for the symmetric SC devices comprising of only ACs.<span><a href="#adma201800963-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201800963-bib-0024_R_d55730399e1793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></span> It is also evident that our device can maintain stable charging/discharging features under normal and deformed states, without any discernible performance decay. With respect to the cycling performance of our device, after 30 cycles of continually bending–flat tests, it manages to retain over 80% of energy density (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0005">5</a>c). Apparently, there is no distortion for the charge–discharge curves after the device returned to flat stage (Figure S17, Supporting Information). To demonstrate its potential usage, the as-manufactured Na-HSC device was employed to power a commercial white LED indicator under flat and distinctly bent states (Figure <a href="#adma201800963-fig-0005">5</a>d). Nevertheless, it is noted that there is still big room for the performance of our devices under “flexible” testing conditions, future work by focusing upon the design of advanced device architecture, enhancing the energy density over prolonged lifespan, as well as considering the actual application scenarios would be of great significance to real-life wearable energy storage applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201800963-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ac34ec1c-f2b2-4dd8-8400-39307900e1c0/adma201800963-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ac34ec1c-f2b2-4dd8-8400-39307900e1c0/adma201800963-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d6a2251d-097b-4202-9b40-b56ca5097f47/adma201800963-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ac34ec1c-f2b2-4dd8-8400-39307900e1c0/adma201800963-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201800963-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201800963" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of a bendable Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>//AC Na-HSC device. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the bendable Na-HSC full cell at different bending angles in a potential range of 1.0-4.3 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. c) Cycling performance of the bendable Na-HSC full cell at distinct deformed states. d) Photographs showing that our Na-HSC device could power a white LED indicator under different bent conditions.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In summary, Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites were fabricated by directly growing ultrathin graphene shells over <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires with the aid of PECVD. Such CVD-derived graphene coatings with ample topological defects would also enable fast electron and Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport, guaranteeing rapid pseudocapacitive processes on the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/electrolyte interface. The Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites yield remarkable sodium ion storage property, displaying high-rate capability of ≈130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C. Full-cell Na-HSC further demonstrates an advanced energy output and power delivery, outperforming most of those from recently reported Na-HSC counterparts. Encouragingly, a prototype bendable Na-HSC device is devised based on as-obtained Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and commercial AC cathode, demonstrating stable and durable electrochemical performances under different deformed conditions and after various bending–release cycles. Our design would offer a pathway toward the next generation wearable energy devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201800963-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201800963-sec-0020-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> Nanowires</i>: Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires were synthesized via a simple hydrothermal route. Typically, 16 g NaOH (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.) was dissolved in 40 mL distilled water in ice bath, followed by the addition of 0.24 g of Nb powders (Aladdin, 99.95% metal basis). Under vigorous stirring for 10 min, the obtained solution was poured into a 100 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave. The autoclave was sealed and maintained at 130 °C for 20 h and then naturally cooled to room temperature. The intermediate product was washed three times with Milli-Q water and then immersed in diluted HCl for 10 h. The precipitate was then collected, washed, freeze-dried, and annealed at 700 °C in air for 3 h to produce white-colored <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> Composites</i>: The coating of graphene on Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanowires was realized by a direct PECVD route. Briefly, the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> powders were spread evenly and placed into a CVD tube furnace (1 in.). The system was then vacuumed to a base pressure of 0.1 Pa and purged several times with pure argon to remove oxygen. The tube was then heated to 500 °C in an argon atmosphere (50 sccm). A 80 W radio frequency plasma was generated at the entrance of the CVD furnace. Upon experiencing a low-temperature (500 °C) CVD reaction for 30–60 min in a mixed gas flow of Ar/CH<sub>4</sub> (volume ratio 5:1), the obtained dark grey powders were collected and directly used for further electrochemical tests and device fabrications.</p>
         
         <p><i>Characterization</i>: Morphologies of samples were observed by the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) using a Hitachi S-4800 operated at accelerating voltage of 5–15 kV. The microstructures of samples were analyzed by TEM (FEI Tecnai F20; acceleration voltage 200 kV) and aberration-corrected atomic-resolution TEM (FEI Titan 300–80; 80 kV). The crystallinity of samples was identified by X-ray diffraction (Bruker D8 Advance Diffractometer) using Cu-K<sub>α</sub> radiation (λ = 1.5406 Å). The diffraction angle (2θ) was stepped between 10° and 80° with an increment of 3° min<sup>−1</sup>. Raman spectroscopy was conducted on a JobinYvon LabRAM HR 800 instrument with 514.5 nm excitations. The XPS spectra were obtained using an Escalab 250Xi Spectrophotometer.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element"><i>Electrochemistry</i>: Commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes were prepared by casting slurries containing 70 wt% active materials, 10 wt% sodium alginate (Aldrich), and 20 wt% carbon black (Timcal, Switzerland) in Milli-Q water onto 10 µm thick copper foils (99.999% purity) by a doctor blade method. The laminates were then punched into circle discs of ≈1.33 cm<sup>2</sup> and thoroughly dried at 120 °C under vacuum for 12 h prior to use. The mass loading of the commercial Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was typically controlled at ≈1–1.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> in all electrodes. The electrochemical performance was measured with CR2032-type coin cells assembled in an argon-filled glove box (&lt;0.01 ppm of oxygen and water). Sodium foil was employed as the counter electrode and Whatman GF/F glass microfiber filter was adopted as the separator. The electrolyte used in electrochemical cells was composed of 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt in in EC:dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1 in v:v) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate additive. Galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling of all three electrodes was performed on a LAND CT2001A battery testing system between 0.01 and 3 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, and the charge–discharge rates were calculated with respect to the theoretical capacity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> based on a four-electron reaction (96 500 × 4/(3.6 × M<sub>Nb2O5</sub>) = 403 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). For the rate capability tests, all electrodes were discharged (sodiation) and charged (desodiation) at the same rate till 20 C (8.06 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Cyclic voltammetry in the potential windows of 0.01 and 3 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at various scan rates from 0.1 to 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup> was performed on an Autolab potentiostat (Autolab Instruments, Netherland). A Na-HSC full cell was constructed with a Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anode and an activated carbon cathode with an active materials weight ratio of 1:4. The activated carbon electrode was prepared casting slurries containing 90 wt% active materials, 5 wt% polyvinylidene fluoride, and 5 wt% super P conductive carbon blacks in Milli-Q water onto 15 µm thick aluminum foils (99.999%) by doctor blade method. Before assembling the full cells, the Gr-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode was precycled three times at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup> in a Na half-cell, and then sodiated to 0.01 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. In Na-HSC full-cell tests, the current densities were based on the total mass of both active anode and cathode materials. The energy and power densities of full-cells were calculated by numerically integrating the galvanostatic discharge profiles using the equations below
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201800963-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/8e38047f-a6bc-4b4f-b9bf-c5f41bef20c5/adma201800963-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201800963-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/72bfaffe-23de-4ff1-a3c5-563f9291d008/adma201800963-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201800963:adma201800963-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>I</i> is the charge/discharge current, <i>V</i> is the working voltage, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> are the start/end-of-discharge time (s), respectively, and <i>t</i> corresponds to the discharge time.</div>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adma201800963-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span><span class="author">V. Etacheri</span>, <span class="author">R. Marom</span>, <span class="author">R.
